Reward for Returning a Runaway Slave: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on reward amount when slave's value < 40 dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the reward amount when a slave's value is less than 40 dirhams, explain their reasoning, and identify the practical difference resulting from their dispute.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf insists on the fixed 40 dirham reward; Muhammad adjusts it to preserve the master's benefit.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement between two great students of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. At issue is the amount of reward for returning a runaway slave when the slave's value falls below the standard 40 dirhams. Understanding their different methodological commitments reveals how textual evidence and purpose-based reasoning interact in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the common ground
Both scholars agree that the reward (ja'alah) is wajib upon the master when the slave is returned, and that the default amount prescribed by the hadith is 40 dirhams.
2
Present Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf holds that the returner receives the full 40 dirhams regardless of the slave's value, because the amount is fixed by explicit text (nas) and cannot be reduced.
He also argues that settlement for more than 40 is invalid, but settlement for less is permissible as a waiver.
3
Present Muhammad's view
Muhammad holds that the returner receives the slave's value minus one dirham (e.g., if value is 30, give 29). His reasoning focuses on the purpose (maqsad) of the reward: to incentivize return while also providing some benefit to the master.
The reduction of one dirham ensures the master gains something from the return.
4
Analyze the key difference
The dispute centers on whether the textual fixity (nas) overrides the underlying purpose (maqsad) when the value is lower. Abu Yusuf prioritizes the text; Muhammad prioritizes the purpose.
Both are valid methods within Hanafi usul.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the slave is worth 30 dirhams, Abu Yusuf would award 40 dirhams, while Muhammad would award 29 dirhams. This directly affects the master's financial obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَ إِنْ كَانَتْ قِيْمَتُهُ أَقَلَّ مِنْ أَرْبَعِيْنَ يُقْضَى لَهُ بِقِيْمَتِهِ إِلَّا دِرْهِما قَالَ وَهَذَا قَولُ مُحَمَّدٌ وَ قَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ لَهُ أرْبَعُونَ دِرُهما لأَنَّ التَّقْدِيرَ بِهَا ثَبَتَ بِالنَّصِ فَلَا يُنْقَصُ عَنْهَا وَلِهَذَا لَا يَجُوزُ الصُّلْحُ عَلَى الزِّيَادَةِ بِخِلَافِ الصُّلْحُ عَلَى الأقل لأنَّهُ حَلٌّ مِنْهُ وَلِمُحَمَّد أَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ حَمْلُ الْغَيْرِ عَلَى الرَّدْ لِيُحْيِي مَالَ الْمَالِكِ فَيَنْقُصَ دِرْهُمْ لِيُسَلَّمَ لَهُ شَيْ تَحْقِيقًا لِلْفَائِدَةِ
ترجمہ اور اگر اس غلام کی قیمت چالیس درہم سے بھی کم ہو تو غلام واپس کرتے وقت چالیس سے ایک درہم کم دے۔ مصنف رحمتہ اللہ علیہ نے کہا ہے کہ یہ قول امام محمد رحمہ اللہ علیہ کا ہے اور امام ابو یوسف رحمتہ اللہ علیہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ لانے والے کو چالیس درہم ہی ملیں گے کیونکہ یہ مقررہ مقدار نص حدیث سے ثابت ہے لہذا اس سے کم نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ اسی بناء پر اگر با ہم دونوں متفق ہو کر چالیس درہم سے بھی زیادہ خرچ کے لین دین پر متفق ہو جائیں تو بھی جائز نہ ہو گا۔ اس کے برخلاف اس سے کم پر جائز ہوگا اس وجہ سے کہ لانے والے نے اپنے حق سے کچھ معاف کر دیا ہے اور امام محمد رحمتہ اللہ علیہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جعل کا مقصود یہ ہے کہ لانے والے کو ایک غیر شخص کا غلام واپس پہنچانے پر آمادہ کرنا ہے تا کہ مالک کا اصل مال تباہ و برباد نہ ہو لہذ ا مال کی اصل قیمت سے ایک درہم کم دیا جائے ۔ تا کہ اس کو بھی کچھ فائدہ حاصل ہو ۔
He (al-Quduri) said: And if its value is less than forty, he is awarded its value except one dirham. He said: This is the statement of Muhammad. And Abu Yusuf said: He gets forty dirhams because the specification of that amount is established by the text, so it cannot be reduced. For this reason, a settlement for more than forty is not permissible, unlike a settlement for less, because the returner has waived some of his right. Muhammad's argument is that the purpose is to induce others to return the slave to revive the master's property, so he reduces one dirham so that some benefit is achieved for him (the master). Translation: And if the value of this slave is even less than forty dirhams, then at the time of returning the slave, give forty less one dirham. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said that this is the statement of Imam Muhammad. And Imam Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said that the returner will receive exactly forty dirhams because this fixed amount is established by the text of hadith, so it cannot be reduced. Similarly, if both parties agree on a transaction of more than forty dirhams, it is not permissible. On the contrary, less than that is permissible because the returner has waived some of his right. Imam Muhammad's proof is that the purpose of the reward is to induce a third party to return the slave so that the master's property is not destroyed; therefore, one dirham is reduced so that the master also benefits.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the case when the value of the runaway slave is less than 40 dirhams. Imam Muhammad holds that the returner should be given the slave's value minus one dirham (e.g., if value is 30 dirhams, give 29). Imam Abu Yusuf insists on the full 40 dirhams regardless, because the fixed amount is established by the text (nas) and cannot be reduced. Abu Yusuf further argues that if the parties agree on more than 40, it is not valid (since the amount is fixed), but agreeing on less is valid because it is a waiver by the returner. Muhammad counters that the purpose of the reward is to incentivize people to return slaves, and by reducing one dirham, the master also gains some benefit (he pays less than the slave's value). This dispute is recorded in the Hidayah and the commentary explains both positions.

Key Terms
نص (nas): The explicit text of the hadith that specifies the 40 dirham reward.
جعل (ja'alah): The reward for returning a runaway slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling according to Imam Muhammad when the slave's value is less than 40 dirhams?
A) The returner gets the full 40 dirhams.
B) The returner gets the slave's value minus one dirham.
C) The returner gets nothing.
D) The returner gets the slave's value exactly.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, Abu Yusuf adheres strictly to the textual amount of 40 dirhams, while Muhammad adjusts the amount to one dirham less than the slave's value to preserve the purpose of the reward.

Ja'alah for Umm Walad and Mudabbar
States that umm walad and mudabbar are like ordinary slaves for ja'alah during master's lifetime

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on ja'alah for returning a runaway umm walad or mudabbar based on the timing of return.

Core Idea
Ja'alah for umm walad and mudabbar is due only during the master's lifetime; after death, they are free.

This lesson clarifies a specific point about the reward (ja'alah) for returning runaway slaves of special categories. The umm walad (slave woman who bore her master's child) and mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred until the master's death) have future rights to freedom, which affects whether a ja'alah is owed to the returner. The rule distinguishes between return during the master's lifetime, where the slave is treated like an ordinary slave, and return after his death, where no reward is given because the slave has become free.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two slave categories
Note that umm walad and mudabbar are distinct from a qin (ordinary slave) because they become free upon the master's death.
2
Determine the timing of return
The ruling depends on whether the return occurs during the master's lifetime or after his death.
3
Apply the rule
If the return is during the master's lifetime, ja'alah is due as for a qin. If after death, no ja'alah is due because the slave is no longer owned.
4
Compare with an ordinary slave (qin)
A qin does not become free upon the master's death, so ja'alah would still be due if returned after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا اَمُّ الْوَلَدِ وَالمُدَبَّ فِى هذا بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْقِنِ إِذَا كَانَ الرَّدُّ فِي حَيَوةِ الْمَوْلَى لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ إِحْيَاءِ مِلْكِهِ وَلَوْرَةٌ بَعْدَ مَمَاتِهِ لَا جُعَلَ فِيهِمَا لِأَنَّهُمَا يُعْتَقَانِ بِالْمَوْتِ بِخِلَافِ القِنْ
ترجمہ اور اگر مولی کی وفات کے بعد واپس لایا تو ام ولد یامد بر ہونے کی صورت میں اسے کچھ بھی جعل ( خرچ ) نہیں ملے گا کیونکہ یہ دونوں ہی مولی کی موت کے بعد آزاد ہو جاتے ہیں۔ بخلاف خالص غلام کے کہ وہ اس کے مرنے کے بعد آزاد نہیں ہوتا ہے۔
And as for umm walad and mudabbar, in this matter they are like a qin (ordinary slave) if the return is during the master's lifetime, because it revives his ownership. But if after his death, there is no ja'alah for them because they become free upon death, unlike the qin. Translation: And if it is returned after the master's death, then in the case of umm walad or mudabbar, the returner will receive no ja'alah (expense) because both of them become free after the master's death, unlike a pure slave who does not become free after his death.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that the umm walad (a slave woman who bears a child from her master) and the mudabbar (a slave whose manumission is deferred until the master's death) are treated like ordinary slaves (qin) with respect to the ja'alah for returning them if they are returned during the master's lifetime. This is because during his lifetime, his ownership over them is active. However, if they are returned after the master's death, the returner gets no reward because upon the master's death, both the umm walad and the mudabbar become free (the umm walad is free upon the master's death according to some, and the mudabbar by definition). Therefore, there is no longer any master's ownership to revive, so no ja'alah is due. This is in contrast to an ordinary slave (qin) who does not become free upon the master's death and thus the ja'alah would still be due if returned after death.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child from her master; she becomes free upon her master's death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred until the master's death.
قِن (qin): An ordinary slave with no special status regarding freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a returner receive ja'alah for returning a runaway mudabbar?
A) Only if returned after the master's death.
B) Only if returned during the master's lifetime.
C) Always, regardless of timing.
D) Never, because mudabbar is not owned.

Key Takeaway
The ja'alah for returning a runaway umm walad or mudabbar is due only if the return occurs during the master's lifetime; after his death, no reward is given because both become free.

Family Members and Ja'alah Exception
Exception to ja'alah for close relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the family members exempt from ja'alah and explain the customary basis for the exception.

Core Idea
Family ties presume gratuitous service: no ja'alah for father, dependent son, or spouse.

We have seen that the master must pay ja'alah to a stranger who returns a runaway slave. However, when the returner is a close family member, the ruling changes due to prevailing custom. This passage from Al-Hidayah specifies the family members and the reasoning behind the exception.

Guided Path
1
List the exempted relatives
Identify the three categories: the master's father, the master's son who is under his care, and either spouse for the other.
The son must be dependent (ft 'ālihi).
2
Understand the customary basis
Recognize that in society, family members return property out of goodwill, not for reward. This custom (urf) qualifies the general rule of ja'alah.
The phrase 'al-āda' (custom) is the key operative reason.
3
Apply the exception
If any of these relatives returns the slave, no ja'alah is obligatory for the master. The master may still give a gift, but it is not compulsory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ كَانَ الرادابا المولى أو ابنهُ وَهُوَ فِي عَالِهِ أَوْ أَحَدُ الزَّوْجَيْنِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ فَلَا جُعَلَ لِأَنَّ هُوَ لا يَتَبَرَّعُونَ بِالرَّدْعَادَةً وَلَا يَتَنَا وَلَهُمْ إِطْلَاقُ الْكِتَابِ
ترجمہ اور اگر پکڑ کر لانے والا شخص غلام کے مالک کا باپ یا بیٹا ہو جو اس کی ذمہ داری اور عیالداری میں ہو یا شوہر اور اس کی بیوی میں سے کوئی ایک دوسرے کا غلام پکڑ لائے تو کچھ بھی اس کا خرچ اور جعل نہیں ملے گا ۔ کیونکہ لوگوں میں یہ معاملہ جاری ہے اور یہی عادت ہے کہ اس موقع پر لانے والا بطور احسان کے لاتا ہے کسی مالی لالچ کی بناء پر نہیں لاتا ہے۔ اس لئے کتاب کا حکم جو مطلق ہے ان لوگوں کو شامل نہ ہو گا۔
And if the returner is the master's father or his son who is in his care, or one of the spouses for the other, then there is no ja'alah because they are not expected to return it as a favor, and it is not... and the absoluteness of the Book applies to them. Translation: And if the person who captures and brings the slave is the master's father or son who is under his responsibility and care, or one of the spouses brings the other's slave, then he will receive no expense or ja'alah. Because among people, this matter is established, and the custom is that on such an occasion, the returner brings it as a favor, not for financial greed. Therefore, the absolute ruling of the Book (the general rule) does not include these people.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage establishes an exception to the general rule of ja'alah. When the person returning the runaway slave is a close family member of the master (father, dependent son, or spouse), no compensation is due. The reason is that customarily these individuals would return a family member's property as a favor, not expecting a reward. Therefore, the general ruling that requires payment of ja'alah does not apply to them. The phrase 'the absoluteness of the Book' refers to the general textual evidence (probably the hadith) that prescribes ja'alah, but it is qualified by the custom that family members act gratuitously.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'alah): The reward for returning a runaway slave.
عارِف (عُرْف) ('urf): Custom; the prevailing practice among people that qualifies legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who among the following is NOT entitled to ja'alah for returning a runaway slave?
A) A stranger.
B) The master's mother.
C) The master's father.
D) The master's neighbor.

Key Takeaway
Family ties and custom override the general obligation to pay ja'alah when the returner is the master's father, dependent son, or spouse.

Returner's Liability for Second Escape
States that the returner is an amīn and not liable for re-escape or death, with an analogy to the seller.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the returner's liability if the runaway slave re-escapes or dies, and explain the qiyas to a seller's right of detention.

Core Idea
The returner of a runaway slave is an amīn, not liable for re-escape or death without negligence.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific scenario: after catching a runaway slave, the returner loses it again before delivery. Imam al-Marghinani rules that the returner is not liable, using two key concepts: the trust (amāna) status and an analogy to a seller's right to detain goods until payment. This lesson unpacks the proofs and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the act (returning a runaway slave) and what is the explicit ruling when the slave runs away again? The text states 'nothing is upon him'.
The ruling is non-liability.
2
Locate the Dalil
Two proofs are given: first, the slave is an amāna (trust) in his hand; second, an analogy (qiyas) to a seller who detains the sold item until payment.
The amāna is the primary basis; the qiyas reinforces the right to detain.
3
Understand the Condition
The non-liability applies only if the returner had made witnesses to his intention to return the slave. This establishes the trust status clearly.
Without witnesses, the trust might not be established and liability could arise.
4
Examine the Qiyas
The returner is likened to a seller: the seller may detain the sold item until payment; the returner may detain the slave until he receives the ja'alah (reward). Similarly, if the slave dies, the returner is not liable.
The illa (effective cause) is the right to secure compensation.
5
Apply to Death Scenario
The ruling is extended: if the slave dies in the returner's custody, he is also not liable, because he is an amīn or because he detained lawfully.
This confirms the amāna principle covers both escape and death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ أَبِقَ مِنَ الذِي رَدَّهُ فَلَا شَيْءٌ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّهُ أُمَانَةٌ فِي يَدِهِ لَكِنْ هَذَا إِذَاشَهِدَ وَقَدْ ذَكَرْنَاهُ فِي الْقَطَةِ قَالَ وَذَكَرَ فِي بَعْض النسخ أنَّهُ لَا شَيْءَ لَهُ وَهُوَ صَحِيح أيضًا لأنَّهُ فِى مَعْنَى الْبَائِعِ مِنَ الْمَالِكِ وَلِهَذَا كَانَ لَهُ أَنْ يَحْبِسَ الإِبِقَ حَتَّى يَسْتَوْفِى الْجُعَلَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْبَائِعِ يَحْبِسُ الْمَسِيعَ لِإِستَيْقَاءِ الشَّمَنِ وَ كَذَالِكَ إِذَامَاتَ فِي يَدِهِ لَا شَيء عليه لما قلنا
ترجمہ کیا اور اگر غلام اس شخص کے ہاتھ سے بھاگ گیا جو اسی بھاگے ہوئے کو اس کے مالک کے پاس پہنچانے کے لئے لا رہاتھا تو اس لانے پر کوئی جرمانہ نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ وہ غلام اس وقت اس کے قبضہ میں امانت کے طور پر تھا۔ البتہ شرط یہ ہوگی کہ اس وقت جبکہ اس غلام کو پکڑنا چاہ رہاتھا کسی کو اس بات پر گواہ بنا دیا ہو کہ میں اس کے مالک کے پاس واپس پہنچانے کے لئے پکڑ رہا ہوں ۔ مصنف حمتہ اللہ علیہ نے اس کو لفظ کے مسائل میں بیان کر دیا ہے۔ پھر مصنف رحمتہ اللہ علیہ نے یہ فرمایا کہ قدوری رحمتہ اللہ علیہ کے بعض نسخوں میں یہی مذکور ہے کہ واپس لانے والے کے لئے کچھ نہیں ہے اور یہ نسخہ بھی سی ہے یعنی جب اس کے پاس سے غلام بھاگ گیا تو اس کو کچھ جعل ( خرچ ) نہیں ملے گا کیونکہ وہ مالک کے ہاتھ میں بیچنے والے کے معنی میں ہے۔ یعنی وہ جب تک مالک کے حوالہ اس غلام گو نہ کر دے اس وقت تک وہ کسی خرچ کے پانے کا مستحق نہیں ہو گا۔ اسی لئے واپس لانے والے کو یہ اختیار ہے کہ اپنا جعل ( مطالبہ حق ) وصول کر لینے تک بھگوڑے غلام کو اپنے پاس ہی رہنے ے۔ جیسے کہ بیچنے والے کو یہ اختیار ہوتا ہے کہ اپنی قیمت نقد وصول کر لینے تک وہ مال یا سامان خریدار کو دینے سے روک دے۔ اسی طرح اگر واپس لانے والے کے قبضہ میں وہ غلام مر جائے تو بھی اس پر کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا یعنی وہ غلام یا اس کی قیمت کا ضامن نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وہ اس وقت تک امین ہے یا اس نے اپنا حق وصول کر لینے کے لئے روکا ہے۔
He said: And if the slave runs away from the one who returned him, then nothing is upon him because it is an amana in his hand. But this is if he had testified (made witnesses) and we have mentioned it in the chapter of luqatah. He said: And it is mentioned in some copies that nothing is due to him, and this is also correct because he is in the position of a seller from the master. For this reason, he may detain the runaway slave until he receives the ja'alah, like the seller detains the sold item until he receives the price. Similarly, if the slave dies in his hand, nothing is upon him due to what we said. Translation: He (the author) said: If the slave runs away from the person who was bringing him to his master, then there is no penalty for that bringing, because the slave at that time was in his possession as a trust. However, the condition is that at the time he intended to catch the slave, he made someone witness that he is catching it to return to its master. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) has explained this in the issues of al-Luqatah. Then the author said that in some copies of al-Quduri, it is mentioned that the returner gets nothing, and this copy is also sound: meaning when the slave runs away from him, he will receive no ja'alah because he is in the position of the seller from the master. That is, as long as he does not hand over the slave to the master, he is not entitled to any expense. For this reason, the returner has the right to keep the runaway slave with him until he receives his ja'alah (claim of right), just as the seller has the right to withhold the sold item until he receives the price in cash. Similarly, if that slave dies in the possession of the returner, then nothing is wajib upon him, meaning he will not be a guarantor of the slave or its price, because he is an amin at that time, or he has detained it to receive his right.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the runaway slave, after being caught by the returner, runs away again from the returner before being delivered to the master. The ruling is that the returner is not liable because the slave was in his possession as an amana (trust). However, this only applies if the returner had made witnesses to the fact that he was catching the slave for the purpose of returning it to the master (to establish his intention). The author (al-Marghinani) notes that in some recensions of al-Quduri, it is stated that the returner gets nothing (i.e., loses his right to ja'alah) because he is like a seller: he must actually deliver the slave to the master to become entitled to the reward. Therefore, the returner has the right to detain the slave until he receives the ja'alah, analogous to a seller detaining the sold item until payment. If the slave dies in the returner's custody, he is not liable because he is either an amin (trustee) or he detained the slave lawfully to secure his right. The passage clarifies that the returner's liability is limited; he is not a guarantor for the slave's safety beyond negligence.

Key Terms
أمانة (amana): A trust; the slave in the returner's possession is a trust, so no liability for loss unless negligence.
بائع (bai'): Seller; the returner is compared to a seller who withholds the item until payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what right does the returner have regarding the runaway slave before receiving ja'alah?
A) He must hand over immediately.
B) He may detain the slave until ja'alah is paid.
C) He cannot detain the slave.
D) He must free the slave.

Key Takeaway
A returner of a runaway slave is an amīn and is not liable for re-escape or death, and he may detain the slave until his reward is paid, analogous to a seller.

No Reward for Returning Umm Walad or Mudabbar After Master's Death
Ruling with contrast between slave types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reward for returning an umm walad or mudabbar after the master's death and explain the legal basis for the distinction from a regular slave.

Core Idea
Returning a slave after the owner's death earns reward only if the slave remains property after death.

It may seem that any return of a runaway slave should deserve a reward, but Hanafi law distinguishes based on the slave's legal status at the time of return. This lesson clarifies why an umm walad or mudabbar, who become free upon the master's death, do not trigger the reward (ju'l), while a pure slave does. Understanding this ruling requires knowing the posthumous status of these slave categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the slave type
Consider the scenario: a person returns a runaway slave after the master has died. Determine whether the returned slave is an umm walad, a mudabbar, or a pure regular slave (ghulam khalis).
This classification is decisive for the ruling.
2
State the ruling for umm walad and mudabbar
If the returned slave is an umm walad or mudabbar, the returner is not entitled to any reward (ju'l). This is the clear Hanafi position.
The reason is that these two categories become free upon the master's death.
3
Contrast with a pure slave
If the returned slave is a pure slave (neither umm walad nor mudabbar), the returner does receive the reward, because the pure slave remains in ownership of the estate after the master's death.
This contrast highlights the underlying legal principle.
4
Explain the basis
The ruling is based on the fact that umm walad and mudabbar are set free automatically at the moment of the master's death. Returning them after that point is not returning property, but rather returning a free person, which does not merit a reward.
The reward (ju'l) is compensation for returning property to its owner, and here the owner no longer owns the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ اور اگر مولی کی وفات کے بعد واپس لایا تو ام ولد یا مدبر ہونے کی صورت میں اسے کچھ بھی جعل (خرچ) نہیں ملے گا کیونکہ یہ دونوں ہی مولی کی موت کے بعد آزاد ہو جاتے ہیں۔ بخلاف خالص غلام کے کہ وہ اس کے مرنے کے بعد آزاد نہیں ہوتا ہے۔
Translation: And if (the slave) is brought back after the master's death, in the case of an umm walad or mudabbar, he will not receive any reward (reimbursement), because both of these become free after the master's death. Contrary to a pure slave, who does not become free after his death.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: If a person returns a runaway slave who is an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne her master's child) or a mudabbar (a slave whose manumission is deferred to after the master's death) after the master has died, the returner is not entitled to any reward (ju'l). The reason is that both types of slaves become free automatically upon the master's death, so returning them after death is not returning a slave but returning a free person. This is distinguished from a regular slave (ghulam khalis) who remains a slave after the master's death and becomes part of the estate. Thus, returning a regular slave after death still warrants reward. The ruling is straightforward and based on the legal status of these slaves: they are no longer property after the master's death.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child of her master, and is consequently freed upon his death.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death.
جعل (ju'l): Reward or compensation given to a person who returns a runaway slave to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because the slave becomes free upon the master's death and is no longer owned property
C) Because the master cannot own a slave after death
D) Because the reward is only due for returning slaves who have run away, not after death

Key Takeaway
The ruling hinges on the slave's status at the time of return: free (umm walad/mudabbar) or still owned (pure slave); only the latter entitles a reward.

No Reward for Close Relatives as Returners
Ruling on no reward for close relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which family members are excluded from receiving reward (ju'l) for returning a runaway slave and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on custom.

Core Idea
Custom precludes reward: close relatives’ returns are favors, not paid services.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling: when a father, a dependent son, or a spouse returns a runaway slave, they are not entitled to the reward (ju'l). The reasoning rests on custom (urf) and the limitation of the general text. Understanding this helps you see how Hanafi fiqh uses custom to qualify general rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the excluded persons
List the three categories: the master's father, the master's son living as a dependent (in his household), and either spouse returning a slave of the other.
The son must be under the master's maintenance (ʻiyāl) to be excluded.
2
Understand the reason: custom (urf)
These individuals do not ordinarily expect payment for such acts; they perform them out of familial duty. Thus, the custom excludes them from reward.
Custom (urf) qualifies the general application of the text.
3
Apply the principle: itlaq al-kitab is restricted
The general ruling (from Quran or Sunnah) that grants reward does not include these cases because custom shows they are not intended beneficiaries.
Hanafi usul: absolute text may be restricted by prevalent practice.
4
Recall the ruling
No reward (ju'l) is due from any of these relatives. The act is not considered a compensable transaction.
The ruling is mufta bihi (operative) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ كَانَ الرَّادُ أَبَا الْمَوْلَى أَوِ ابْنَهُ وَهُوَ فِي عِيَالِهِ أَوْ أَحَدَ الزَّوْجَيْنِ عَلَى الْآخَرِ فَلَا جُعَلَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُمْ لَا يَتَبَرَّعُونَ بِالرَّدِّ عَادَةً وَلَا يَتَنَاوَلُهُمْ إِطْلَاقُ الْكِتَابِ
And if the returner is the father of the master, or his son while he is in his household, or one of the spouses (returning) from the other, then there is no reward for him, because they do not perform such returns as a favor by custom, and the absolute application of the book (the ruling) does not include them.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: Certain individuals do not qualify for the reward (ju'l) when they return a runaway slave. These include: (1) the father of the master, (2) the son of the master who lives in the master's household (i.e., dependent), (3) either spouse returning a slave of the other spouse. The reason is that in such relationships, returning a runaway is considered a customary favor, not a commercial transaction. People in these bonds do not expect payment; they do it out of family duty. Therefore, the general ruling of the book (which grants reward) does not apply to them. This ruling is based on custom (urf) and the principle that the absolute text (itlaq al-kitab) is qualified by prevalent practice.

Key Terms
عِيَال (iyal): Dependents or household members; here specifically the son who is under the father's maintenance.
جُعَل (ju'l): Reward for returning a runaway slave.
إطْلَاقُ الْكِتَابِ (itlaq al-kitab): The general or absolute application of a legal ruling from the primary texts (Qur'an/Sunnah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT entitled to reward (ju'l) for returning a runaway slave in Hanafi law?
A) A stranger who returns the slave
B) The master's father
C) The master's dependent son
D) A neighbor who returns the slave

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, close family members (father, dependent son, spouses) are excluded from reward for returning a runaway slave because custom treats such returns as familial duty, not a commercial transaction.

Returner's Liability and the Condition of Witnessing
Ruling with proof: returner's non-liability for escape/death conditional on witnessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the returner's liability when a slave escapes or dies, and explain the condition of witnessing and its legal basis.

Core Idea
No liability for returner if he witnessed — he is a trustee, not a guarantor.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario in the fiqh of runaway slaves. The returner, who brings the slave back to the owner, may worry about being held liable if the slave escapes again or dies. The Hanafi school clarifies that as long as the returner has properly witnessed his intention to return, he is considered a trustee (amin) and bears no liability. The reasoning extends from the laws of lost property (luqatah) and draws an analogy to a seller's right to detain goods until payment. Let us unpack the conditions and the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for each scenario
The passage gives rulings for escape and death. Identify that in both cases the returner is not liable, provided he witnessed.
The same reasoning applies to both: the slave is an amanah.
2
Recognize the condition
The condition is that the returner must have taken witnesses (ishhad) when he took the slave. Without this, his status changes.
This condition is taken from the chapter of luqatah.
3
Trace the legal basis
The reason given: the slave is an amanah (trust) in his hand. This is established by analogy to luqatah.
The finder of lost property is also a trustee if he takes with witnesses.
4
Understand the analogy to the seller
The returner also has a right to detain the slave until he receives his reward (ju'l), similar to a seller detaining the sold item for the price.
This reinforces his position as a trustee, not a guarantor.
5
Note the variant view
The passage mentions some copies state he gets no reward if he didn't witness, but that is also correct.
This is a minor intra-Hanafi nuance, not affecting the liability ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنْ أَبِقَ مِنَ الَّذِي رَدَّهُ فَلَا شَيْءَ عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّهُ أَمَانَةٌ فِي يَدِهِ لَكِنْ هَذَا إِذَا شَهِدَ وَقَدْ ذَكَرْنَاهُ فِي الْقَطَةِ قَالَ وَذَكَرَ فِي بَعْضِ النُّسَخِ أَنَّهُ لَا شَيْءَ لَهُ وَهُوَ صَحِيحٌ أَيْضًا لِأَنَّهُ فِي مَعْنَى الْبَائِعِ مِنَ الْمَالِكِ وَلِهَذَا كَانَ لَهُ أَنْ يَحْبِسَ الْإِبِقَ حَتَّى يَسْتَوْفِيَ الْجُعَلَ بِمَنْزِلَةِ الْبَائِعِ يَحْبِسُ الْمَبِيعَ لِاسْتِيفَاءِ الثَّمَنِ وَكَذَلِكَ إِذَا مَاتَ فِي يَدِهِ لَا شَيْءَ عَلَيْهِ لِمَا قُلْنَا.
He said: And if the (slave) runs away from the one who returned him, then nothing is upon him because it is a trust in his hand. But this is when he witnessed (his intention). And we have mentioned it in the chapter of lost property. He said: And it is mentioned in some copies that he has nothing (of reward) and it is also correct because he is in the meaning of a seller from the owner. And for this reason, he may detain the runaway slave until he receives the reward, analogously to a seller detaining the sold item for receipt of the price. Similarly, if the slave dies in his hand, nothing is upon him, for what we have said.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage discusses the liability of the person who is returning a runaway slave (al-radd). It addresses three scenarios: (1) If the slave runs away from the returner, the returner is not liable for compensation because the slave is in his possession as a trust (amanah), not as a guarantor. However, this protection is conditional upon the returner having taken witnesses at the time he took the slave, declaring his intention to return it to the owner. This condition is mentioned in the chapter of luqatah (lost property). (2) If the returner did not take witnesses, then according to some copies (of al-Quduri's work), he gets no reward and is also not liable for loss? The text says 'it is also correct' that he gets no reward; the reasoning is that his situation is analogous to a seller: the seller has the right to detain the sold item until receipt of payment. Similarly, the returner may detain the slave until he receives his reward. (3) If the slave dies in the returner's hand, he is similarly not liable, as it remains a trust. The overall principle is that the returner is an agent-amanah, not a guarantor, provided he witnesses his intention. The discussion includes a comparison to the seller's right of detention (habs al-mabi') for payment.

Key Terms
أمَانَة (amanah): A trust; possession without liability for loss unless negligent.
إِشْهَاد (ishhad): Taking witnesses to declare one's intention to return the slave to the owner.
جُعَل (ju'l): Reward for returning the runaway.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the returner of a runaway slave not liable if the slave runs away from him?
A) If he knew the slave's owner
B) If he took witnesses of his intention to return the slave
C) If he did not accept any reward
D) If the slave was not injured

Key Takeaway
The returner of a runaway slave is not liable for escape or death if he has properly witnessed his intention; his possession is an amanah.

Reward Due When Master Manumits or Sells Runaway Upon Meeting
Ruling that reward is due if master manumits or sells slave upon meeting returner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the reward becomes due if the master manumits or sells the runaway slave upon meeting the returner, and explain the qiyas reasoning.

Core Idea
Manumission or sale of the runaway slave by the master upon meeting the returner constitutes constructive possession, making the reward wajib.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific situation in the law of runaway slaves (abiq). When the returner brings the slave back, the master may not physically take possession but might manumit or sell the slave upon seeing the returner. The question is: does the returner still deserve his reward? The Hanafi answer is yes, based on the concept of constructive possession (qabd hukmi) and qiyas to the case of a purchaser who manumits before physical delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
The passage presents two distinct actions by the master upon meeting the returner and the slave: (1) manumitting the slave, and (2) selling the slave back to the returner. In both cases, the reward (ju'l) becomes due.
The common element is that the master does not physically take the slave but performs an act that implies ownership and possession.
2
Understand Constructive Possession (Qabd Hukmi)
In Hanafi fiqh, possession is not always physical. Manumission by the owner is considered a form of constructive possession because it affirms ownership and control. Similarly, selling the slave to the returner ensures the price, making the transaction valid and the reward due.
This concept is key: the returner's possession is deemed transferred to the master through the master's own act.
3
Apply the Qiyas to the Purchaser Analogy
The text explicitly analogizes to a purchaser who manumits a slave before physically taking possession. In that case, the purchaser is considered to have taken possession through the manumission. Here, the master's manumission serves the same function, so the returner's reward is payable.
The analogy is qiyas jali (clear analogy): the illa is the act of manumission indicating possession.
4
Distinguish Return from a Regular Sale
The return of a runaway slave has the legal effect of a sale but is not a full sale. Specifically, it does not fall under the prohibition of selling something before possession (bai' ma lam yuqbad). Therefore, the master can validly sell the slave to the returner upon meeting, and the reward is due.
This nuance prevents the transaction from being void due to the prohibition.
5
Conclude the Ruling
In both scenarios, the reward becomes wajib immediately upon the master's action, regardless of physical delivery. The returner is entitled to the promised compensation.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَوِ اعْتَقَهُ الْمَوْلَى كَمَا لَقِيَهُ صَارَ قَابِضًا بِالْعِتَاقِ كَمَا فِي عَبْدِ الْمُشْتَرَى وَكَذَا إِذَا بَاعَهُ مِنَ الرَّادِّ لِسَلَامَةِ الْبَدَلِ لَهُ وَالرَّدُّ وَإِنْ كَانَ لَهُ حُكْمُ الْبَيْعِ لَكِنَّهُ بَيْعٌ مِنْ وَجْهٍ فَلَا يَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ النَّهْيِ الْوَارِدِ عَنْ بَيْعِ مَا لَمْ يُقْبَضْ فَجَازَ.
He said: And if the master manumits him when he meets him, he becomes a possessor by the manumission, just as in the case of a purchased slave. Likewise, if he sells him to the returner, because the compensation is safe for him. And the return, although it has the ruling of a sale, is a sale only in one respect, so it does not fall under the prohibition of selling something not yet possessed, so it is permissible.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage discusses two scenarios where the master's action upon meeting the returner and the slave results in the reward being due: (1) If the master manumits the slave upon seeing him (without physically taking possession), the master is deemed to have taken constructive possession (qabd hukmi) through the act of manumission. This is analogous to a purchaser who manumits a slave immediately after purchase; he is considered to have taken possession. Therefore, the returner is entitled to his reward because the master has effectively accepted the delivery. (2) If the master sells the slave back to the returner (i.e., the returner buys the slave), the reward is also due because the price is ensured. The reasoning: although the return of a runaway slave has the legal effect of a sale (in terms of transfer of ownership), it is not a complete sale in all respects. Specifically, it does not fall under the prohibition of selling something before taking possession (bai' ma lam yuqbad), which normally invalidates a sale. Since the returner is already holding the slave, the transaction is valid and the reward is payable. This highlights the nuanced Hanafi distinction between the legal effects of returning a runaway and a regular sale.

Key Terms
قَبْض حُكْمِيّ (qabd hukmi): Constructive possession; legal possession without physical handling, e.g., by manumission or accepting delivery from seller.
جُعَل (ju'l): Reward for returning the runaway.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if the master, upon meeting the returner, sells the runaway slave back to the returner?
A) The reward is forfeited because the slave is no longer with the master.
B) The reward is due because the sale ensures the compensation and is valid.
C) The reward is postponed until the slave is physically delivered to the master.
D) The reward is halved because the returner now owns the slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the master's manumission or sale of the runaway slave upon meeting the returner counts as constructive possession, making the returner's reward wajib.

Witnessing Required for Reward on Returning a Runaway Slave
Ruling that witness is necessary for reward on returning a runaway slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement of witnessing for reward when returning a runaway slave and apply the principle that omission of witnessing indicates personal intent.

Core Idea
Taking witnesses at the time of taking a runaway slave is decisive for reward; without it, no reward.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a subtle but critical procedural point in the chapter on runaway slaves. The returner's intention must be transparent at the outset. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad teach that merely returning the slave is insufficient to claim the reward (ju'al); the returner must have declared his intention through witnesses at the very moment he took the slave. This lesson unpacks why witnessing is legally necessary and how its absence changes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its exceptions
The passage states that witnessing (ishhad) at the time of taking is 'befitting' (yanbaghi) and that failure to witness results in no reward according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Note the exception: if one buys the slave with a witness that he buys to return, then reward is due.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (amarah)
The lack of witnessing is treated as an 'amarah' (indication) that the taker took the slave for himself, not for the owner. This is a form of circumstantial evidence in Hanafi usul.
This is an example of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy), where intent is inferred from an external sign.
3
Analyze the analogous cases
The text analogizes the situation without witness to purchasing, receiving as a gift, or inheriting the slave and then returning. In all these cases, the return is considered a personal act (li nafsihi), so no reward.
These analogies help confirm the rule that the reward is only for those who act as agents of the owner from the start.
4
Distinguish the exception
If the taker buys the slave and takes witnesses that he buys to return, then he gets reward even though he paid the price. He is considered a mutabarri' (volunteer) in paying the price, but the witnessing proves his agency.
This shows that the key is the declaration of intent at the time of acquisition, not the mode of acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَيَنْبَغِي إِذَا أَخَذَهُ أَنْ يَشْهَدَ أَنَّهُ يَأْخُذُهُ لِيَرُدَّهُ فَإِنْ لَمْ يَشْهَدْ فَلَا جُعَلَ لَهُ عَلَى قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ. لَوْ رَدَّهُ مَنْ لَمْ يَشْهَدْ وَقْتَ الْأَخْذِ لَا جُعَلَ لَهُ عِنْدَهُمَا لِأَنَّ تَرْكَ الْإِشْهَادِ أَمَارَةٌ أَنَّهُ أَخَذَهُ لِنَفْسِهِ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا اشْتَرَاهُ مِنَ الْآخِذِ أَوِ اهْتَبَهُ أَوْ وَرِثَهُ فَرَدَّهُ عَلَى مَوْلَاهُ لَا جُعَلَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُ رَدَّهُ لِنَفْسِهِ إِلَّا إِذَا شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ اشْتَرَاهُ لِيَرُدَّهُ فَيَكُونَ لَهُ الْجُعَلُ وَهُوَ مُتَبَرِّعٌ فِي أَدَاءِ الثَّمَنِ.
He said: And it is befitting that when he takes him, he should witness that he takes him to return him. For if he does not witness, then no reward is due for him according to the statement of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. If one who did not witness at the time of taking returns (the slave), no reward is due in their view because the omission of witnessing is an indication that he took him for himself. And it becomes as if he bought him from the taker, or received him as a gift, or inherited him, then returned him to his master: he gets no reward, because he returned him for himself. Except if he witnessed that he bought him to return him, then he gets the reward, and he is a volunteer (mutabarri') in paying the price.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage emphasizes the importance of witnessing (ishhad) when the returner initially takes the runaway slave. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the two major imams of the school), if the returner does not take witnesses at the time he takes the slave, declaring his intention to return him to the master, then even if he later returns the slave, he is not entitled to any reward. The reason is that the lack of witnessing is considered an indication (amarah) that he took the slave for himself, not for the owner. This would be similar to several scenarios: (a) if he bought the slave from the person who found it (without intention to return), (b) if he received it as a gift, (c) if he inherited it, and then later decided to return it to the original master — in all these cases, the return is considered a personal act, not a service for the owner, so no reward is due. However, there is an exception: if he purchased the slave explicitly with a witness that he bought it to return to the owner, then he is entitled to the reward. In such a case, he is considered to have volunteered (mutabarri') in paying the price (i.e., he bears the cost personally, but gets the reward). This ruling is specific to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad; Abu Yusuf may have had a different view. The passage guides the procedure for a returner to secure his right to reward.

Key Terms
إِشْهَاد (ishhad): Taking witnesses to declare one's intention to return the slave.
أَمَارَة (amarah): An indication or circumstantial evidence that influences legal judgment.
مُتَبَرِّع (mutabarri'): One who performs an act voluntarily without expectation of compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling on a person who takes a runaway slave without witnesses and later returns him to the master?
A) He gets the full reward
B) He gets half the reward
C) He gets no reward
D) He can still claim reward if the master agrees

Key Takeaway
Witnessing at the time of taking a runaway slave is essential for reward; without it, the returner is presumed to have acted for himself and forfeits the reward.

Runaway Slave as Pledge: Reward on Mortgagee
Ruling with brief rationale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who bears the reward for returning a pledged runaway slave, and explain the rationale based on benefit.

Core Idea
When a runaway slave is a pledge, the mortgagee pays the reward because he revives its value for his own benefit.

This lesson presents a concise ruling from the Hanafi school: if a slave held as a pledge (rahn) runs away, the reward (ju'l) for returning him falls on the mortgagee (murtahin). The reasoning is based on benefit — the mortgagee gains from the return as the slave secures his debt. Understanding this rule illustrates the Hanafi principle that he who benefits bears the cost.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
Identify the owner (master) of the slave, the slave himself, and the mortgagee (creditor) who holds the slave as a pledge for a debt.
The slave is a pledge (rahn) — meaning it is security for a loan.
2
State the ruling
The reward (ju'l) paid to the person who returns the runaway slave is wajib upon the mortgagee, not the owner.
This is the specific Hanafi position.
3
Explain the rationale
The mortgagee is obligated because he revives the financial value of the pledge by its return. The value benefits him since the slave secures his debt.
The Arabic uses 'ahya maliyyatahu' (he revived its financial value).
4
Connect to the general principle
This ruling aligns with the broader Hanafi principle that he who derives direct benefit from an act bears its cost.
Compare to other cases where benefit determines liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَبِقُ رَهْنًا فَالْجُعَلُ عَلَى الْمُرْتَهِنِ لِأَنَّهُ أَحْيَا مَالِيَّتَهُ بِالرَّدِّ وَهِيَ حَقُّهُ إِذَا الِاسْتِيفَاءُ مِنْهَا وَالْجُعَلُ
And if the runaway is a pledge (rahn), then the reward is upon the mortgagee (murtahin), because he revived its financial value by the return, and it is his right since collection (of debt) is from it, and the reward (is due).
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage states a ruling for a specific case: when the runaway slave is already held as a pledge (rahn) for a debt. In this situation, the reward (ju'l) for returning the runaway is payable by the mortgagee (murtahin), not the owner. The reason is that the mortgagee benefits from the return because the slave secures his debt. By returning the slave, the returner is essentially preserving the value of the pledge, which serves the mortgagee's interest. The phrase 'he revived its financial value' (ahya maliyyatahu) indicates that the return brings the asset back into the creditor's security. Since the mortgagee can recover his debt from the pledged slave, he is the primary beneficiary and thus must pay the reward. This ruling follows the general Hanafi principle that the person who directly benefits from an act bears its cost. Note: this assumes the mortgagee is in possession of the pledge? Actually, in a pledged runaway, the slave is in the mortgagee's possession (or constructively) but has run away. Upon return, the mortgagee regains the benefit.

Key Terms
أَبِق (abiq): Runaway slave.
رَهْن (rahn): Pledge; an item given as security for a debt.
مُرْتَهِن (murtahin): The creditor who receives a pledge; mortgagee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The original owner (master)
B) The mortgagee (creditor)
C) The returner himself
D) The state treasury

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a pledged runaway slave is returned, the mortgagee pays the reward because he revives the value of the pledge for his own benefit.

Freeing or Selling a Runaway Slave to the Retriever
States the ruling and talil for two valid scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the owner freeing or selling a runaway slave to the retriever and explain the talil (legal reasoning) that validates these transactions.

Core Idea
Freeing or selling to the retriever creates constructive possession, securing the reward.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses the legal consequences when the owner of a runaway slave either frees the slave upon meeting the retriever or sells the slave to the retriever himself. The text explains why both acts are valid and why the retriever is still entitled to the ju'l (reward). The reasoning hinges on the concept of constructive possession, analogous to the case of a purchased slave freed immediately, and on the special nature of the return transaction, which is considered a 'sale from one aspect' and thus exempt from the prohibition of selling before possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
The passage presents two distinct actions by the owner upon meeting the retriever: (1) freeing the slave, (2) selling the slave to the retriever. For each, the ruling is that the retriever's right to the reward is preserved.
Both scenarios assume the retriever has already captured the slave but has not yet physically returned him to the owner.
2
Analyze the First Scenario: Freeing
The owner frees the slave immediately upon meeting. The text says the owner becomes a 'possessor' (qābiḍ) by the act of freeing, drawing an analogy to a purchased slave whom the buyer frees right after purchase. In that analogy, the buyer takes possession through the contract and then releases; here, the owner's freeing confirms his ownership and thereby his possession, so the retriever's prior capture is recognized as lawful possession for the purpose of the reward.
The analogy (qiyās) is to the case of 'abd al-mushtarā (purchased slave) where immediate freeing validates the buyer's possession.
3
Analyze the Second Scenario: Selling
The owner sells the slave to the retriever himself. The text argues that since the payment (thaman) is secure for the owner (i.e., the price is guaranteed), the transaction is valid. Although the return (radd) has the ruling of a sale, it is a sale 'from one aspect' – a mixed transaction combining return-of-captive and sale. Therefore, it does not fall under the prohibition of selling what one has not taken possession of (because the retriever already has possession by capture).
The general hadith prohibition applies only to pure sales where the seller has not yet received possession; here, the retriever already holds the slave.
4
Extract the Principle: Constructive Possession
Both scenarios rely on the idea that lawful possession can be established without physical handover: through freeing (which is an act of ownership) or through the ongoing possession of the retriever from the moment of capture. The reward (ju'l) is a compensation for the service of returning, not a sale price, so the rules of sale are applied only partially.
This principle allows flexibility in rewarding finders and capturers without strict contract formalities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَلَوِ اعْتَقَهُ الْمَوْلى كَمَا لَقِيَهُ صَارَ قَابِضاً بِاعْتِاقِهِ كَمَا فِي عَبْدِ المُشْتَرى وَكَذَا إِذَا بَاعَهُ مِنَ الرَّادِ لِسَلَامَةِ الْبَدَلِ لَهُ وَالرَّدُّ وَإِنْ كَانَ لَهُ حُكْمُ البَيْعِ لَكِنَّهُ بَيْعٌ مِنْ وَجْهٍ فَلا يَدْخُلُ تَحْتَ النَّهَى الْوَارِدِ عَنْ بَيْعِ مَالَمْ يَقْبِضْ فَجَازَ
He said: And if the master freed him as soon as he met him, he becomes a possessor by his freeing, just as in the case of a purchased slave. And likewise if he sold him to the retriever, because the payment is secure for him. And the return, even though it has the ruling of a sale, is a sale from one aspect, so it does not fall under the prohibition against selling what one has not taken possession, so it is permissible.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the Hidayah on the reward for returning a runaway slave. It addresses two scenarios: first, if the owner frees the slave immediately upon meeting him, then the owner is deemed to have taken possession of the slave by the act of freeing (since freeing confirms ownership), and thus the retriever is entitled to the reward (ju'l). This is analogous to a purchased slave whom the buyer frees immediately – in that case the buyer becomes owner and must pay the price. Second, if the owner sells the slave to the retriever himself, the sale is valid even though the retriever does not physically possess the slave at the time of contract. This is because the transaction is not a pure sale but a combination of return and sale; the prohibition of selling what one has not taken possession of does not apply here as the retriever has already taken possession by capturing.

Key Terms
جعل (ju'l): Reward or compensation given to the person who returns a runaway slave.
راد (radd): The person who returns the runaway slave to the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is selling the runaway slave to the retriever valid despite the general prohibition of selling before possession?
A) Because the retriever already has possession by capturing the slave.
B) Because the owner has freed the slave first.
C) Because the sale is for a price less than the slave's value.
D) Because the owner and retriever have a pre-existing contract.

Key Takeaway
Freeing or selling to the retriever creates constructive possession, securing the retriever's right to the reward.

Witnesses Required for Reward on Returned Slave
Explains the need for witnesses when capturing the slave to secure the reward

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of witnessing for reward eligibility when capturing a runaway slave and apply the analogy to similar transactions.

Core Idea
No witnesses at capture means no reward – intent is judged by outward signs.

The retrieval of a runaway slave can earn a reward (ju'l), but not automatically. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the retriever must openly declare his intention at the moment of capture by having witnesses. This lesson unpacks that condition, the consequence of neglecting it, and the exceptional case where witnessing later can still secure the reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Required Condition
Pinpoint the act in question (taking a runaway slave) and the condition attached (bearing witnesses that the taking is for the purpose of returning).
The text uses 'yambaghi' (it is appropriate), but the loss of reward elevates witnessing to a necessary condition.
2
Understand the Consequence of Omission
If the retriever fails to witness at the time of capture, he receives no reward upon returning the slave. The omission is taken as evidence that he intended to keep the slave for himself.
This is the core ruling: reward is tied to manifesting the correct intent at the outset.
3
Examine the Analogy
The text compares the witness-less retriever to someone who buys, receives as a gift, or inherits the slave and then returns him. In all those cases, the return is for personal benefit, so no reward is due.
The analogy shows that the presence or absence of a declared returning-intent at acquisition is decisive.
4
Note the Exception
If someone purchases the slave and at that time witnesses that he buys to return him, then he qualifies for the reward, even though he is considered a volunteer regarding the price.
Witnessing at purchase can retroactively (in effect) establish the intent, but only if done at the moment of acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَيَنْبَغِي إِذَا أَخَذَهُ أَنْ يَشْهَدَ أَنَّهُ يَأْخُذُهُ لِيَرُدَّهُ فَإِنَّ الشَّهَادَةَ فِيهِ عَلَيْهِ عَلَى قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ لَوْ رَدَّهُ مَنْ لَمْ يَشْهَدْ وَقْتَ الْأَخْذِ لَا جُعَلَ لَهُ عِنْدَهُمَا لِأَنَّ تَرْكَ الْإِشْهَادِ أَمَارَةٌ أَنَّهُ أَخَذَهُ لِنَفْسِهِ وَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا اشْتَرَاهُ مِنَ الْأَخِذِ أَوِ اتَّهَبَهُ أَوْ وَرِثَهُ فَرَدَّهُ عَلَى مَوْلَاهُ لَا جُعَلَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُ رَدَّهُ لِنَفْسِهِ إِلَّا إِذَا شَهِدَ أَنَّهُ اشْتَرَاهُ لِيَرُدَّهُ فَيَكُونَ لَهُ الْجُعَلُ وَهُوَ مُتَبَرِّعٌ فِي أَدَاءِ الثَّمَنِ
He said: And it is appropriate that when he takes him, he should bear witness that he takes him to return him. For indeed, bearing witness regarding him is upon him according to the saying of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. If one who did not bear witness at the time of taking returns him, there is no reward for him according to them, because leaving off bearing witness is an indication that he took him for himself. And he becomes like one who bought him from the taker, or received him as a gift, or inherited him, then returned him to his master – there is no reward for him, because he returned him for himself. Except when he bears witness that he bought him to return him, then he has the reward, and he is a volunteer in paying the price.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah discusses the requirement of witnesses for entitlement to the reward (ju'l) for returning a runaway slave. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, it is recommended (yambaghi) that the retriever declare and bear witness at the time of capture that he is taking the slave solely for the purpose of returning him to his owner. If he fails to do so and later returns the slave, he is not entitled to any reward because the absence of witnessing indicates that he intended to keep the slave for himself. The text draws an analogy: if someone purchases, receives as a gift, or inherits a slave from the original retriever and then returns him, he gets no reward because the return is for his own benefit. However, an exception is made if he explicitly witnesses at the time of purchase that he is buying to return – then he qualifies for the reward, though he is considered a volunteer (mutabarra’) regarding the price paid. This ruling emphasizes the importance of manifesting intent.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): Act of making witnesses testify to one's intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if someone captures a runaway slave without witnesses and then returns him, is he entitled to the reward?
A) Yes, because returning is what matters.
B) No, because lack of witnesses indicates he intended to keep the slave.
C) Yes, but only if the owner agrees.
D) No, unless he later gets witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A reward for returning a runaway slave requires witnesses at the time of capture to prove the intent to return.

Ruling on Reward for Returning a Pawned Runaway Slave
Determines who bears the reward for a pawned runaway slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on who bears the reward for returning a pawned runaway slave and apply the proportional allocation rule when the slave's value exceeds the debt.

Core Idea
The mortgagee pays the reward because he benefits; if value exceeds debt, he pays proportionally.

This passage addresses a nuanced case within pawn (rahn) contracts: a pawned slave runs away and is returned. Who should pay the reward? The ruling hinges on financial interest and is derived by analogy to expenses for medical treatment or ransom. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi fiqh allocates costs based on proportional benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary obligation
The mortgagee (creditor) is responsible for the reward because he revives his collateral, which secures his debt.
The reward is obligatory (wajib) on the mortgagee, not the pawner.
2
Distinguish two scenarios
Rule changes based on whether the slave's value is equal to or less than the debt, or greater than the debt.
When value ≤ debt, mortgagee bears full reward. When value > debt, allocation is proportional.
3
Apply the rule of proportionality
If value exceeds debt, the mortgagee pays only to the extent of his secured interest (i.e., debt/value ratio). The pawner pays the remainder.
This is analogous to sharing costs of medicine or ransom according to ownership interest.
4
Recognize the basis in qiyas
The ruling is derived via qiyas (analogy) from the principle that costs follow benefit. The illa (effective cause) is financial benefit.
The analogy is to a case where multiple owners share expenses proportionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَبِقُ رَهْنًا فَالْجُعَلُ عَلَى الْمُرْتَهِنِ لِأَنَّهُ أَحْيَا مَالِيَّتَهُ بِالرَّدِّ وَهِيَ حَقُّهُ إِذِ الْاسْتِيفَاءُ مِنْهَا فَيَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ وَالرَّدُّ فِى حَيَوةِ الرَّاهِنِ وَبَعْدَهُ سَوَاءٌ لِأَنَّ الرَّهْنَ لَا يَبْطُلُ بِالْمَوْتِ وَهَذَا إِذَا كَانَ قِيمَتُهُ مِثْلَ الدَّيْنِ أَوْ أَقَلَّ مِنْهُ فَإِنْ كَانَتْ أَكْثَرَ فَبِقَدْرِ الدَّيْنِ عَلَيْهِ وَالْبَاقِي عَلَى الرَّاهِنِ لِأَنَّ حَقَّهُ بِالْقَدْرِ الْمَضْمُونِ فَصَارَ كَتَمَنِ الدَّوَاءِ وَتَخْلِيْصِهِ عَنِ الْحِمَايَةِ بِالْفِدَاءِ
If the runaway slave is a pawn, then the reward is upon the mortgagee, because he revived his wealth by returning it, and it is his right since satisfaction (of debt) is from it. So it is upon him. And the return is the same during the life of the pawner and after his death, because the pawn is not invalidated by death. This is when its value is equal to the debt or less. If it is more, then proportionate to the debt is upon him, and the remainder upon the pawner, because his right is to the extent of the secured amount. So it becomes like the cost of medicine and ransoming him from captivity by ransom.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage deals with a runaway slave that has been pawned (rahn). The reward for returning him is to be paid by the mortgagee (murtahin), not the pawner (rahin), because the mortgagee benefits from the return as it revives the collateral securing his debt. The same ruling applies whether the pawner is alive or dead, since pawnage does not terminate upon death. However, the reward is only borne fully by the mortgagee if the slave's value is equal to or less than the debt. If the slave's value exceeds the debt, the mortgagee pays only a portion proportional to the debt, and the pawner pays the rest. This is analogized to spending on medicine for a sick slave or ransoming him from captivity, where expenses are shared proportionally according to interest.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): Pawn; a slave given as collateral for a debt.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who holds the pawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a pawned runaway slave is worth 1000 dirhams and the debt is 400 dirhams, who bears the reward?
A) The pawner pays all.
B) The mortgagee pays all.
C) The mortgagee pays for 400 dirhams' worth, the pawner for 600.
D) The reward is split equally.

Key Takeaway
The reward for returning a pawned runaway slave is paid by the mortgagee proportionally to his secured interest, not by the pawner alone.

Priority of Reward Over Debts for Runaway Slave
States the ruling on reward priority for an indebted runaway slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of payment when a runaway slave with debts is sold and explain the Hanafi rationale based on ownership being suspended.

Core Idea
When a runaway slave with debts is sold, the reward is paid first because it is a cost of ownership—ownership is suspended until debts are cleared.

This lesson examines the ruling on a runaway slave (abiq) who also owes personal debts (madbun). The Hanafi school establishes a clear priority: the reward for capturing the slave must be paid from the sale price before other creditors. The reasoning ties to the nature of ownership, which is temporarily suspended due to the debts. Let's walk through the two scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the master's two options
The master may either repay the slave's debts to keep him, or sell the slave to satisfy the debts.
The reward is due on the master when he repays; if he sells, the reward is taken from the proceeds.
2
Apply the payment order when selling
When the slave is sold, the reward (ju'l) is paid first from the sale price; the remainder goes to the creditors.
The reward functions as an expense of ownership, like maintenance costs.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The reward is considered mu'nat al-milk (maintenance of ownership). The slave's ownership is mawquf (suspended) until debts are settled. Thus, the reward is obligatory on the one who ultimately becomes the owner.
This prioritizes the reward as a necessary expense of securing the property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَانَ مَدْبُونًا فَعَلَى الْمَوْلَى إِنِ اخْتَارَ قَضَاءَ الدَّيْنِ وَإِنْ بَيْعَ بُدِئَ بِالْجُعَلِ وَالْبَاقِي لِلْغُرَمَاءِ لِأَنَّهُ مُونَةُ الْمِلْكِ وَالْمِلْكُ فِيهِ كَالْمَوْقُوفِ فَيَجِبُ عَلَى مَنْ يَسْتَقِرُّ لَهُ الْمِلْكُ
And if he (the slave) is indebted, then the reward is upon the master if he chooses to repay the debt. And if he is sold, one begins with the reward, and the remainder is for the creditors, because it is a maintenance expense of ownership, and ownership in him is like a suspended trust. So it is obligatory upon the one for whom ownership is established.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This brief passage addresses a runaway slave who also has personal debts (madbun). The reward for capturing him is initially the responsibility of his master (mawla) if the master chooses to pay off the slave's debts and retain him. If the master opts not to pay, the slave is sold to satisfy his debts. In that case, the reward (ju'l) is paid first from the sale proceeds, and the remaining amount goes to the creditors. The reasoning is that the reward is like a maintenance cost (mu'nat al-milk) of ownership, and the slave's ownership is treated as suspended (mawquf) until debts are settled. Thus, the reward falls on the one who ultimately becomes the owner after debt resolution.

Key Terms
مدبون (madbun): A slave who owes debts.
جعل (ju'l): Reward or compensation for capturing a runaway slave.
مونة الملك (mu'nat al-milk): Maintenance expense of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a runaway slave with debts is sold, what is the order of payment from the price?
A) Creditors first, then reward.
B) Reward first, then remaining to creditors.
C) Equal distribution among all claimants.
D) Owner gets first priority.

Key Takeaway
The reward takes priority over the slave's debts because it is a cost of ownership that attaches to the suspended ownership.

Who Bears the Ransom?
Determines liability for a runaway criminal slave's ransom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the reward for returning a runaway criminal slave falls on the party that chooses to keep or take the slave.

Core Idea
The reward follows the benefit: whoever chooses to keep the slave bears the ransom.

This lesson addresses a nuanced scenario in slave law: when a runaway slave commits a crime and is captured, who is liable for the ransom? The answer depends on the choice of the involved parties, and the ruling is tied to the principle that the reward goes to the one who benefits from the slave's return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject
The subject is a runaway slave (janib) who has committed a crime such as homicide or battery. Determine the status: the slave is in the hands of the finder or authorities.
The term janib specifically refers to a slave with criminal liability.
2
State the two options
Two parties can choose: the master may ransom the slave (pay fida, blood money) to redeem him, or the victim's heirs may demand the slave himself (daf') for retribution or as compensation.
3
Apply the ruling per option
If the master chooses ransom, the reward (the liability for the ransom) is upon the master. If the heirs choose to take the slave, the reward is upon them. The text states this clearly.
The reward is the obligation to pay or bear the ransom.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The rationale is that the benefit of the slave returns to the one who makes the choice. The master benefits by keeping the slave, the heirs benefit by gaining ownership (or retribution).
This mirrors the broader Hanafi principle that liability follows enjoyment of benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَانَ جَانِبًا فَعَلَى الْمَوْلَى إِنِ اخْتَارَ الْفِدَاءَ لِعَوْدِ الْمَنْفَعَةِ إِلَيْهِ وَعَلَى الْأَوْلِيَاءِ إِنِ اخْتَارَ الدَّفْعَ لِعَوْدِهَا إِلَيْهِمْ
And if he is a criminal, then (the ransom) is upon the master if he chooses ransom, because the benefit returns to him. And upon the heirs (of the victim) if they choose handing over (the slave), because the benefit returns to them.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This final passage addresses a runaway slave who has committed a crime (janiba) such as murder or injury. If the master chooses to pay the ransom (fida) to redeem the slave (i.e., pay blood money), then he bears the reward for capturing as well? Actually the context is that the reward for returning is upon the one who ultimately benefits. The master benefits by keeping the slave, so if he opts to ransom, the reward is on him. If the victim's heirs choose to take the slave in retribution (daf'), then the reward is on them because the slave becomes their property. The exact wording is 'on the master if he chooses ransom, and on the heirs if they choose handing over'. This mirrors the logic that the reward is borne by the one who ends up with ownership or benefit of the slave.

Key Terms
جانب (janib): A slave who has committed a crime (especially homicide or battery).
فداء (fida): Ransom paid to redeem the slave from the finder or state.
دفع (daf'): Handing over the slave to the victim's heirs as compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the master of a runaway criminal slave chooses ransom, on whom is the reward?
A) The master.
B) The victim's heirs.
C) The finder of the slave.
D) No one.

Key Takeaway
The reward for a runaway criminal slave is borne by the party that chooses to keep or take the slave, based on who benefits from the slave's return.

Ju'l for a Fugitive Slave of a Minor
Rules on ju'l payment when the fugitive's owner is a minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for ju'l when a fugitive slave belongs to a minor and explain why the guardian cannot claim the reward.

Core Idea
Ju'l is from the minor's wealth; guardian recovers as duty, not for reward.

This lesson examines a specific application of ju'l (reward for returning a fugitive slave) when the slave belongs to a minor. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a third party who finds the slave and the minor's own guardian. Understanding the underlying reason—expense of ownership versus guardian's fiduciary duty—is essential to apply the rule correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
The slave belongs to a minor (sabī). The question is about who pays the ju'l and whether the guardian (waṣī) can claim it.
2
State the primary ruling
If a third party returns the fugitive slave, the ju'l is wajib from the minor's property.
The text says: 'فَالْجُعْلُ فِي مَالِهِ' — the reward is from his wealth.
3
Understand the reason (ta'lil)
The ju'l is an expense of ownership (مَؤُنَةُ مِلْكِهِ). Since the slave is the minor's property, the cost of retrieving him falls on the minor's estate.
This establishes liability on the minor's wealth, not on the guardian personally.
4
Identify the exception
If the guardian himself returns the slave, he is not entitled to any ju'l.
The text: 'وَإِنْ رَدَّهُ وَصِيُّهُ فَلَا جُعْلَ لَهُ'.
5
Analyze the reason for the exception
The guardian is responsible for managing the minor's affairs. Returning the fugitive slave is part of his duty; he cannot claim a separate reward for doing his own job.
This prevents the guardian from double-dipping or profiting from his custodial role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَانَ لِصَبِيٍّ فَالْجُعْلُ فِي مَالِهِ لِأَنَّهُ مَؤُنَةُ مِلْكِهِ وَإِنْ رَدَّهُ وَصِيُّهُ فَلَا جُعْلَ لَهُ لِأَنَّهُ هُوَ الَّذِي يَتَوَلَّى الرَّدَّ فِيهِ
ترجمہ: اور اگر یہ بھگوڑا غلام کسی نابالغ کا ہو تو اس غلام کا جعل اسی بچہ کے مال میں لازم ہوگا۔ کیونکہ یہ جعل تو اس کے ملک پر خرچ آیا ہوا ہے۔ اور اگر اس نابالغ کا وصی اسے واپس لے کر آیا ہو تو اس وصی کو اس کا جعل نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ اس غلام کو ڈھونڈھ کر لے آنا اس کی ذاتی ذمہ داری ہے۔
And if [the slave] belongs to a minor, then the reward is from his wealth because it is an expense of his ownership; but if his guardian returns him, then there is no reward for him because he is the one who undertakes the return of him.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This ruling addresses a fugitive slave owned by a minor (sabi). The Hanafi position: the ju'l for returning the slave is paid from the minor's property, as it is an expense of ownership. However, if the minor's guardian (wasi) personally retrieves the slave, he is not entitled to a ju'l because it is his duty as guardian to manage the minor's affairs—the return is part of his responsibility. The guardian cannot claim a separate reward for performing his own obligation. This distinguishes the case where a third party returns the slave (who is entitled to a ju'l) from the guardian's own return.

Key Terms
صَبِي (sabī): A minor child who has not reached puberty; owns property but is under guardianship.
وَصِي (waṣī): Guardian (executor) appointed to manage the minor's property and affairs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a third person returns a fugitive slave owned by a minor, from whose property is the ju'l paid?
A) The guardian's personal property
B) The minor's property
C) The finder's property
D) The state treasury

Key Takeaway
Ju'l for a fugitive slave owned by a minor is wajib from the minor's wealth; the guardian is not entitled to ju'l for personally returning the slave because it falls within his duty.
